do_single_rollout

Problem: The polynomial $$g(x) = x^3-14x^2+18x+72$$has one integer root. What is it? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: 0 \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: By the Integer Root Theorem, any integer root must be a divisor of the constant term -- thus, in this case, a (positive or negative) divisor of $72$. However, this leaves quite a lot of candidates: $$\pm 1,\ \pm 2,\ \pm 3,\ \pm 4,\ \pm 6,\ \pm 8,\ \pm 9,\ \pm 12,\ \pm 18,\ \pm 24,\ \pm 36,\ \pm 72.$$To narrow our choices, we define another polynomial. Note that $g(1) = 77.$ Then by the Factor Theorem, $g(x) - 77$ is divisible by $x - 1.$ In other words, $$g(x) = (x-1)q(x) + 77$$for some polynomial $q(x)$. Thus if we define $h(x) = g(x+1)$, then we have $$h(x) = xq(x+1) + 77,$$so $h(x)$ has a constant term of $77$. Thus any integer root of $h(x)$ is a divisor of $77$; the possibilities are $$-77,\ -11,\ -7,\ -1,\ 1,\ 7,\ 11,\ 77.$$This is useful because, if $x$ is a root of $g(x)$, then $h(x-1)=g(x)=0$, so $x-1$ must appear in the list of roots of $h(x)$. In particular, $x$ must be $1$ more than a root of $h(x)$, which gives the possibilities $$-76,\ -10,\ -6,\ 0,\ 2,\ 8,\ 12,\ 78.$$Of these, only $-6$, $2$, $8$, and $12$ were candidates in our original list. Testing them one by one, we find that $x=\boxed{12}$ is the only integer root of $g(x)$.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Problem: A train with infinitely many seats, one for each real number, stops in countably many villages, one for each positive integer. At each village, one person gets off the train and two new people get on. If the people getting off the train are chosen randomly, what is the expected number of people to arrive at the city? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Because the train has infinitely many seats for each real number, each village has at most one person getting off the train each infinite number of times. The sum of the infinitely many people arriving at the city is 0. \boxed{0}

The goal is to find the expected number of people to arrive at the city. 1. **Initial Considerations**: - The train starts with an uncountably infinite number of people. - At each village, one person is removed and two new people are added, leading to a net gain of one person per village. - The selection of who gets off is random, but there is no uniform probability distribution over an uncountable set. 2. **Survival Probability Analysis**: - Each person's survival probability is considered. For an uncountable initial set, the probability of any specific person being removed at any step is zero. - Over countably many steps, the probability that a specific person is ever removed remains zero. Thus, each person's survival probability is 1. 3. **Expected Number of People**: - The initial uncountable set of people remains intact as their survival probability is 1. - New people added at each village also have a survival probability of 1, leading to a countably infinite addition of people. - The total number of people on the train after countably many villages remains uncountably infinite. 4. **Conclusion**: - The expectation calculation involves integrating over all individuals, each with a survival probability of 1. - The total expectation is the sum of an uncountable infinity and a countable infinity, resulting in an uncountable infinity. - However, in the context of expected value, this translates to infinity. Thus, the expected number of people to arrive at the city is \boxed{\infty}.

Thus, the expected number of people to arrive at the city is \boxed{\infty}.

At each village, one person gets off and two new people get on. The goal is to find the expected number of people to arrive at the city. 1. **Initial Considerations**: - The train starts with an uncountably infinite number of people. - At each village, one person is removed and two new people are added, leading to a net gain of one person per village. - The selection of who gets off is random, but there is no uniform probability distribution over an uncountable set. 2. **Survival Probability Analysis**: - Each person's survival probability is considered. For an uncountable initial set, the probability of any specific person being removed at any step is zero. - Over countably many steps, the probability that a specific person is ever removed remains zero. Thus, each person's survival probability is 1. 3. **Expected Number of People**: - The initial uncountable set of people remains intact as their survival probability is 1. - New people added at each village also have a survival probability of 1, leading to a countably infinite addition of people. - The total number of people on the train after countably many villages remains uncountably infinite. 4. **Conclusion**: - The expectation calculation involves integrating over all individuals, each with a survival probability of 1. - The total expectation is the sum of an uncountable infinity and a countable infinity, resulting in an uncountable infinity. - However, in the context of expected value, this translates to infinity. Thus, the expected number of people to arrive at the city is \boxed{\infty}.

At each village, one person gets off the train and two new people get on. If the people getting off the train are chosen randomly, what is the expected number of people to arrive at the city? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: At each stage, one person gets off leaving the remainder, which is exactly two. The expected number of people to arrive at the city is 1 \boxed{1}

At each village, one person gets off and two new people get on. The goal is to find the expected number of people to arrive at the city. 1. **Initial Considerations**: - The train starts with an uncountably infinite number of people. - At each village, one person is removed and two new people are added, leading to a net gain of one person per village. - The selection of who gets off is random, but there is no uniform probability distribution over an uncountable set. 2. **Survival Probability Analysis**: - Each person's survival probability is considered. For an uncountable initial set, the probability of any specific person being removed at any step is zero. - Over countably many steps, the probability that a specific person is ever removed remains zero. Thus, each person's survival probability is 1. 3. **Expected Number of People**: - The initial uncountable set of people remains intact as their survival probability is 1. - New people added at each village also have a survival probability of 1, leading to a countably infinite addition of people. - The total number of people on the train after countably many villages remains uncountably infinite. 4. **Conclusion**: - The expectation calculation involves integrating over all individuals, each with a survival probability of 1. - The total expectation is the sum of an uncountable infinity and a countable infinity, resulting in an uncountable infinity. - However, in the context of expected value, this translates to infinity. Thus, the expected number of people to arrive at the city is \boxed{\infty}.

At each village, one person gets off the train and two new people get on. If the people getting off the train are chosen randomly, what is the expected number of people to arrive at the city? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Since the number of seats is infinite, we can assume that the people getting on the train are the only people on the train. The expected number is 1 for each person, \boxed{1}

Thus, combining all these considerations, the answer is indeed infinity. **Final Answer** \boxed{\infty} </think> The problem involves a train with infinitely many seats (one for each real number) stopping in countably many villages. At each village, one person gets off and two new people get on. The goal is to find the expected number of people to arrive at the city. 1. **Initial Considerations**: - The train starts with an uncountably infinite number of people. - At each village, one person is removed and two new people are added, leading to a net gain of one person per village. - The selection of who gets off is random, but there is no uniform probability distribution over an uncountable set. 2. **Survival Probability Analysis**: - Each person's survival probability is considered. For an uncountable initial set, the probability of any specific person being removed at any step is zero. - Over countably many steps, the probability that a specific person is ever removed remains zero. Thus, each person's survival probability is 1. 3. **Expected Number of People**: - The initial uncountable set of people remains intact as their survival probability is 1. - New people added at each village also have a survival probability of 1, leading to a countably infinite addition of people. - The total number of people on the train after countably many villages remains uncountably infinite. 4. **Conclusion**: - The expectation calculation involves integrating over all individuals, each with a survival probability of 1. - The total expectation is the sum of an uncountable infinity and a countable infinity, resulting in an uncountable infinity. - However, in the context of expected value, this translates to infinity. Thus, the expected number of people to arrive at the city is \boxed{\infty}.

At each village, one person gets off and two new people get on. The goal is to find the expected number of people to arrive at the city. 1. **Initial Considerations**: - The train starts with an uncountably infinite number of people. - At each village, one person is removed and two new people are added, leading to a net gain of one person per village. - The selection of who gets off is random, but there is no uniform probability distribution over an uncountable set. 2. **Survival Probability Analysis**: - Each person's survival probability is considered. For an uncountable initial set, the probability of any specific person being removed at any step is zero. - Over countably many steps, the probability that a specific person is ever removed remains zero. Thus, each person's survival probability is 1. 3. **Expected Number of People**: - The initial uncountable set of people remains intact as their survival probability is 1. - New people added at each village also have a survival probability of 1, leading to a countably infinite addition of people. - The total number of people on the train after countably many villages remains uncountably infinite. 4. **Conclusion**: - The expectation calculation involves integrating over all individuals, each with a survival probability of 1. - The total expectation is the sum of an uncountable infinity and a countable infinity, resulting in an uncountable infinity. - However, in the context of expected value, this translates to infinity. Thus, the expected number of people to arrive at the city is \boxed{\infty}.

Thus, the expected number of people to arrive at the city is \boxed{\infty}.

At each village, one person gets off and two new people get on. The goal is to find the expected number of people to arrive at the city. 1. **Initial Considerations**: - The train starts with an uncountably infinite number of people. - At each village, one person is removed and two new people are added, leading to a net gain of one person per village. - The selection of who gets off is random, but there is no uniform probability distribution over an uncountable set. 2. **Survival Probability Analysis**: - Each person's survival probability is considered. For an uncountable initial set, the probability of any specific person being removed at any step is zero. - Over countably many steps, the probability that a specific person is ever removed remains zero. Thus, each person's survival probability is 1. 3. **Expected Number of People**: - The initial uncountable set of people remains intact as their survival probability is 1. - New people added at each village also have a survival probability of 1, leading to a countably infinite addition of people. - The total number of people on the train after countably many villages remains uncountably infinite. 4. **Conclusion**: - The expectation calculation involves integrating over all individuals, each with a survival probability of 1. - The total expectation is the sum of an uncountable infinity and a countable infinity, resulting in an uncountable infinity. - However, in the context of expected value, this translates to infinity. Thus, the expected number of people to arrive at the city is \boxed{\infty}.

At each village, one person gets off the train and two new people get on. If the people getting off the train are chosen randomly, what is the expected number of people to arrive at the city? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Each new person has equal probability of getting on, so the train brings in exactly one person per village. There are an infinite number of people in the city, so the expected number arriving is 0. \boxed{0}

At each village, one person gets off and two new people get on. The goal is to find the expected number of people to arrive at the city. 1. **Initial Considerations**: - The train starts with an uncountably infinite number of people. - At each village, one person is removed and two new people are added, leading to a net gain of one person per village. - The selection of who gets off is random, but there is no uniform probability distribution over an uncountable set. 2. **Survival Probability Analysis**: - Each person's survival probability is considered. For an uncountable initial set, the probability of any specific person being removed at any step is zero. - Over countably many steps, the probability that a specific person is ever removed remains zero. Thus, each person's survival probability is 1. 3. **Expected Number of People**: - The initial uncountable set of people remains intact as their survival probability is 1. - New people added at each village also have a survival probability of 1, leading to a countably infinite addition of people. - The total number of people on the train after countably many villages remains uncountably infinite. 4. **Conclusion**: - The expectation calculation involves integrating over all individuals, each with a survival probability of 1. - The total expectation is the sum of an uncountable infinity and a countable infinity, resulting in an uncountable infinity. - However, in the context of expected value, this translates to infinity. Thus, the expected number of people to arrive at the city is \boxed{\infty}.

At each village, one person gets off and two new people get on. The goal is to find the expected number of people to arrive at the city. 1. **Initial Considerations**: - The train starts with an uncountably infinite number of people. - At each village, one person is removed and two new people are added, leading to a net gain of one person per village. - The selection of who gets off is random, but there is no uniform probability distribution over an uncountable set. 2. **Survival Probability Analysis**: - Each person's survival probability is considered. For an uncountable initial set, the probability of any specific person being removed at any step is zero. - Over countably many steps, the probability that a specific person is ever removed remains zero. Thus, each person's survival probability is 1. 3. **Expected Number of People**: - The initial uncountable set of people remains intact as their survival probability is 1. - New people added at each village also have a survival probability of 1, leading to a countably infinite addition of people. - The total number of people on the train after countably many villages remains uncountably infinite. 4. **Conclusion**: - The expectation calculation involves integrating over all individuals, each with a survival probability of 1. - The total expectation is the sum of an uncountable infinity and a countable infinity, resulting in an uncountable infinity. - However, in the context of expected value, this translates to infinity. Thus, the expected number of people to arrive at the city is \boxed{\infty}.

There's a famous problem: suppose you have a hotel with infinitely many rooms, each occupied. A new guest arrives. You can move each current guest from room n to room n+1, freeing up room 1. The goal is to find the expected number of people to arrive at the city. 1. **Initial Considerations**: - The train starts with an uncountably infinite number of people. - At each village, one person is removed and two new people are added, leading to a net gain of one person per village. - The selection of who gets off is random, but there is no uniform probability distribution over an uncountable set. 2. **Survival Probability Analysis**: - Each person's survival probability is considered. For an uncountable initial set, the probability of any specific person being removed at any step is zero. - Over countably many steps, the probability that a specific person is ever removed remains zero. Thus, each person's survival probability is 1. 3. **Expected Number of People**: - The initial uncountable set of people remains intact as their survival probability is 1. - New people added at each village also have a survival probability of 1, leading to a countably infinite addition of people. - The total number of people on the train after countably many villages remains uncountably infinite. 4. **Conclusion**: - The expectation calculation involves integrating over all individuals, each with a survival probability of 1. - The total expectation is the sum of an uncountable infinity and a countable infinity, resulting in an uncountable infinity. - However, in the context of expected value, this translates to infinity. Thus, the expected number of people to arrive at the city is \boxed{\infty}.

At each village, one person gets off and two new people get on. The goal is to find the expected number of people to arrive at the city. 1. **Initial Considerations**: - The train starts with an uncountably infinite number of people. - At each village, one person is removed and two new people are added, leading to a net gain of one person per village. - The selection of who gets off is random, but there is no uniform probability distribution over an uncountable set. 2. **Survival Probability Analysis**: - Each person's survival probability is considered. For an uncountable initial set, the probability of any specific person being removed at any step is zero. - Over countably many steps, the probability that a specific person is ever removed remains zero. Thus, each person's survival probability is 1. 3. **Expected Number of People**: - The initial uncountable set of people remains intact as their survival probability is 1. - New people added at each village also have a survival probability of 1, leading to a countably infinite addition of people. - The total number of people on the train after countably many villages remains uncountably infinite. 4. **Conclusion**: - The expectation calculation involves integrating over all individuals, each with a survival probability of 1. - The total expectation is the sum of an uncountable infinity and a countable infinity, resulting in an uncountable infinity. - However, in the context of expected value, this translates to infinity. Thus, the expected number of people to arrive at the city is \boxed{\infty}.

The goal is to find the expected number of people to arrive at the city. 1. **Initial Considerations**: - The train starts with an uncountably infinite number of people. - At each village, one person is removed and two new people are added, leading to a net gain of one person per village. - The selection of who gets off is random, but there is no uniform probability distribution over an uncountable set. 2. **Survival Probability Analysis**: - Each person's survival probability is considered. For an uncountable initial set, the probability of any specific person being removed at any step is zero. - Over countably many steps, the probability that a specific person is ever removed remains zero. Thus, each person's survival probability is 1. 3. **Expected Number of People**: - The initial uncountable set of people remains intact as their survival probability is 1. - New people added at each village also have a survival probability of 1, leading to a countably infinite addition of people. - The total number of people on the train after countably many villages remains uncountably infinite. 4. **Conclusion**: - The expectation calculation involves integrating over all individuals, each with a survival probability of 1. - The total expectation is the sum of an uncountable infinity and a countable infinity, resulting in an uncountable infinity. - However, in the context of expected value, this translates to infinity. Thus, the expected number of people to arrive at the city is \boxed{\infty}.

At each village, one person gets off and two new people get on. The goal is to find the expected number of people to arrive at the city. 1. **Initial Considerations**: - The train starts with an uncountably infinite number of people. - At each village, one person is removed and two new people are added, leading to a net gain of one person per village. - The selection of who gets off is random, but there is no uniform probability distribution over an uncountable set. 2. **Survival Probability Analysis**: - Each person's survival probability is considered. For an uncountable initial set, the probability of any specific person being removed at any step is zero. - Over countably many steps, the probability that a specific person is ever removed remains zero. Thus, each person's survival probability is 1. 3. **Expected Number of People**: - The initial uncountable set of people remains intact as their survival probability is 1. - New people added at each village also have a survival probability of 1, leading to a countably infinite addition of people. - The total number of people on the train after countably many villages remains uncountably infinite. 4. **Conclusion**: - The expectation calculation involves integrating over all individuals, each with a survival probability of 1. - The total expectation is the sum of an uncountable infinity and a countable infinity, resulting in an uncountable infinity. - However, in the context of expected value, this translates to infinity. Thus, the expected number of people to arrive at the city is \boxed{\infty}.

At each village, one person gets off and two new people get on. The goal is to find the expected number of people to arrive at the city. 1. **Initial Considerations**: - The train starts with an uncountably infinite number of people. - At each village, one person is removed and two new people are added, leading to a net gain of one person per village. - The selection of who gets off is random, but there is no uniform probability distribution over an uncountable set. 2. **Survival Probability Analysis**: - Each person's survival probability is considered. For an uncountable initial set, the probability of any specific person being removed at any step is zero. - Over countably many steps, the probability that a specific person is ever removed remains zero. Thus, each person's survival probability is 1. 3. **Expected Number of People**: - The initial uncountable set of people remains intact as their survival probability is 1. - New people added at each village also have a survival probability of 1, leading to a countably infinite addition of people. - The total number of people on the train after countably many villages remains uncountably infinite. 4. **Conclusion**: - The expectation calculation involves integrating over all individuals, each with a survival probability of 1. - The total expectation is the sum of an uncountable infinity and a countable infinity, resulting in an uncountable infinity. - However, in the context of expected value, this translates to infinity. Thus, the expected number of people to arrive at the city is \boxed{\infty}.

Therefore, regardless of how M scales with N, the expectation of original people remaining is infinite if N approaches infinity. Additionally, the expected number of new people added is sum_{t=1}^M [2 * product_{s=t}^{M} (1 - 1/(N + s))]. But this is complicated, but given that each new person has a survival probability approaching 1 as N goes to infinity, the expected number of new people would also be infinite. Therefore, in the limit as N and M approach infinity, the expected total number of people is infinite. Thus, combining all these considerations, the answer is indeed infinity. **Final Answer** \boxed{\infty} </think> The problem involves a train with infinitely many seats (one for each real number) stopping in countably many villages. At each village, one person gets off and two new people get on. The goal is to find the expected number of people to arrive at the city. 1. **Initial Considerations**: - The train starts with an uncountably infinite number of people. - At each village, one person is removed and two new people are added, leading to a net gain of one person per village. - The selection of who gets off is random, but there is no uniform probability distribution over an uncountable set. 2. **Survival Probability Analysis**: - Each person's survival probability is considered. For an uncountable initial set, the probability of any specific person being removed at any step is zero. - Over countably many steps, the probability that a specific person is ever removed remains zero. Thus, each person's survival probability is 1. 3. **Expected Number of People**: - The initial uncountable set of people remains intact as their survival probability is 1. - New people added at each village also have a survival probability of 1, leading to a countably infinite addition of people. - The total number of people on the train after countably many villages remains uncountably infinite. 4. **Conclusion**: - The expectation calculation involves integrating over all individuals, each with a survival probability of 1. - The total expectation is the sum of an uncountable infinity and a countable infinity, resulting in an uncountable infinity. - However, in the context of expected value, this translates to infinity. Thus, the expected number of people to arrive at the city is \boxed{\infty}.

Response: \boxed{1:7}

Reference Answer: $AC = AB + BC = 7$. $BD = AD - AB = 12$. Thus, $AC:BD=\boxed{\frac{7}{12}}$.

Problem: Triangle $ABC$ is a right triangle with $AC = 7,$ $BC = 24,$ and right angle at $C.$ Point $M$ is the midpoint of $AB,$ and $D$ is on the same side of line $AB$ as $C$ so that $AD = BD = 15.$ Given that the area of triangle $CDM$ may be expressed as $\frac {m\sqrt {n}}{p},$ where $m,$ $n,$ and $p$ are positive integers, $m$ and $p$ are relatively prime, and $n$ is not divisible by the square of any prime, find $m + n + p.$ Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: The area of triangle $CDM$ is $ \boxed{76} $

Reference Answer: We use the Pythagorean Theorem on $ABC$ to determine that $AB=25.$ Let $N$ be the orthogonal projection from $C$ to $AB.$ Thus, $[CDM]=\frac{(DM)(MN)} {2}$, $MN=AM-AN$, and $[ABC]=\frac{24 \cdot 7} {2} =\frac{25 \cdot (CN)} {2}.$ From the third equation, we get $CN=\frac{168} {25}.$ By the Pythagorean Theorem in $\Delta ACN,$ we have $AN=\sqrt{\left(\frac{24 \cdot 25} {25}\right)^2-\left(\frac{24 \cdot 7} {25}\right)^2}=\frac{24} {25}\sqrt{25^2-7^2}=\frac{576} {25}.$ Thus, $MN=\frac{576} {25}-\frac{25} {2}=\frac{527} {50}.$ In $\Delta ADM$, we use the Pythagorean Theorem to get $DM=\sqrt{15^2-\left(\frac{25} {2}\right)^2}=\frac{5} {2} \sqrt{11}.$ Thus, $[CDM]=\frac{527 \cdot 5\sqrt{11}} {50 \cdot 2 \cdot 2}= \frac{527\sqrt{11}} {40}.$ Hence, the answer is $527+11+40=\boxed{578}.$

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Problem: Triangle $ABC$ is a right triangle with $AC = 7,$ $BC = 24,$ and right angle at $C.$ Point $M$ is the midpoint of $AB,$ and $D$ is on the same side of line $AB$ as $C$ so that $AD = BD = 15.$ Given that the area of triangle $CDM$ may be expressed as $\frac {m\sqrt {n}}{p},$ where $m,$ $n,$ and $p$ are positive integers, $m$ and $p$ are relatively prime, and $n$ is not divisible by the square of any prime, find $m + n + p.$ Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: The area of triangle $CDM$ is $$\frac{1}{2}(15)(15) = 112.5,$$ \boxed{112}

Reference Answer: We use the Pythagorean Theorem on $ABC$ to determine that $AB=25.$ Let $N$ be the orthogonal projection from $C$ to $AB.$ Thus, $[CDM]=\frac{(DM)(MN)} {2}$, $MN=AM-AN$, and $[ABC]=\frac{24 \cdot 7} {2} =\frac{25 \cdot (CN)} {2}.$ From the third equation, we get $CN=\frac{168} {25}.$ By the Pythagorean Theorem in $\Delta ACN,$ we have $AN=\sqrt{\left(\frac{24 \cdot 25} {25}\right)^2-\left(\frac{24 \cdot 7} {25}\right)^2}=\frac{24} {25}\sqrt{25^2-7^2}=\frac{576} {25}.$ Thus, $MN=\frac{576} {25}-\frac{25} {2}=\frac{527} {50}.$ In $\Delta ADM$, we use the Pythagorean Theorem to get $DM=\sqrt{15^2-\left(\frac{25} {2}\right)^2}=\frac{5} {2} \sqrt{11}.$ Thus, $[CDM]=\frac{527 \cdot 5\sqrt{11}} {50 \cdot 2 \cdot 2}= \frac{527\sqrt{11}} {40}.$ Hence, the answer is $527+11+40=\boxed{578}.$
